<p><b>Mississippi</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Mississippi.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/40/En-us-Mississippi.ogg" title="En-us-Mississippi.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">state</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">southeastern region</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Mississippi is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_and_territories_by_area" title="List of U.S. states and territories by area">32nd most extensive</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_population" title="List of U.S. states by population">32nd most populous</a> of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_and_territories_of_the_United_States" title="List of states and territories of the United States">50 United States</a>. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> to the east, the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> to the south, and <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> and Louisiana to the west.  The state's western boundary is largely defined by the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Jackson, Mississippi">Jackson</a>, with a population of approximately 175,000 people, is both the state's <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_municipalities_in_Mississippi" title="List of municipalities in Mississippi">largest city</a>. 
</p><p>The state is heavily forested outside the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a> area, which is the area between the Mississippi and Yazoo rivers. Before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, most development in the state was along riverfronts, as the waterways were critical for transportation. Large gangs of slaves were used to work on cotton plantations. After the war, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedmen" title="Freedmen">freedmen</a> began to clear the bottomlands to the interior, in the process selling off timber and buying property. By the end of the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> made up two-thirds of the Delta's property owners, but timber and railroad companies acquired much of the land after the financial crisis, which occurred when blacks were facing increasing racial discrimination and disfranchisement in the state.
</p><p>Clearing of the land for plantations altered the Delta's ecology, increasing the severity of flooding along the Mississippi by taking out trees and bushes that had absorbed excess waters. Much land is now held by agribusinesses. A largely rural state with agricultural areas dominated by industrial farms, Mississippi is ranked low or last among the states in such measures as health, educational attainment, and median household income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-America's_Health_Rankings_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-America's_Health_Rankings-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The state's <a href="/wiki/Catfish" title="Catfish">catfish</a> <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> farms produce the majority of farm-raised catfish consumed in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1930s and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">Great Migration</a> of African Americans to the North and West, the majority of Mississippi's population has been white, although the state still has the highest percentage of black residents of any U.S. state. From the early 19th century to the 1930s, its residents were majority black, and before the American Civil War that population was composed largely of African-American slaves. <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> whites retained political power through disfranchisement and <a href="/wiki/Jim_Crow_laws" title="Jim Crow laws">Jim Crow laws</a>. In the first half of the 20th century, nearly 400,000 rural blacks left the state for work and opportunities in northern and midwestern cities, with another wave of migration around <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> to West Coast cities. In the early 1960s, Mississippi was the poorest state in the nation, with 86% of its non-whites living below the poverty level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010, 37% of Mississippians were African Americans, the highest percentage of African Americans in any U.S. state. Since regaining enforcement of their voting rights in the late 1960s, most African Americans have supported Democratic candidates in local, state and national elections. Conservative whites have shifted to the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>. African Americans are a majority in many counties of the Mississippi-<a href="/wiki/Yazoo_River" title="Yazoo River">Yazoo</a> Delta, an area of historic slave settlement during the plantation era.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The state's name is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a>, which flows along its western boundary. Settlers named it after the <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe_language" title="Ojibwe language">Ojibwe</a> word <i>misi-ziibi</i> ("Great River").
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1640" data-file-width="2122" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png/300px-Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png/450px-Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png/600px-Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Mississippi_NA.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major highways and waterways in Mississippi</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1157" data-file-width="2981" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg/220px-Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg/330px-Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg/440px-Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wolf-River-swamp-North-Mississippi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bottomland hardwood swamp near <a href="/wiki/Ashland,_Mississippi" title="Ashland, Mississippi">Ashland, Mississippi</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mississippi is bordered to the north by <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, to the east by <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, to the south by <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> and a narrow coast on the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>; and to the west, across the Mississippi River, by Louisiana and <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>.
</p><p>In addition to its namesake, major rivers in Mississippi include the <a href="/wiki/Big_Black_River_(Mississippi)" title="Big Black River (Mississippi)">Big Black River</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pearl_River_(Mississippi-Louisiana)" title="Pearl River (Mississippi-Louisiana)">Pearl River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Yazoo_River" title="Yazoo River">Yazoo River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pascagoula_River" title="Pascagoula River">Pascagoula River</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Tombigbee_River" title="Tombigbee River">Tombigbee River</a>. Major lakes include <a href="/wiki/Ross_Barnett_Reservoir" title="Ross Barnett Reservoir">Ross Barnett Reservoir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arkabutla_Lake" title="Arkabutla Lake">Arkabutla Lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sardis_Lake_(Mississippi)" title="Sardis Lake (Mississippi)">Sardis Lake</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grenada_Lake" title="Grenada Lake">Grenada Lake</a> with the largest lake being Sardis Lake.
</p><p>Mississippi is entirely composed of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lowland" title="Lowland">lowlands</a>, the highest point being <a href="/wiki/Woodall_Mountain" title="Woodall Mountain">Woodall Mountain</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Foothills" title="Foothills">foothills</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Mountains" title="Cumberland Mountains">Cumberland Mountains</a>, 807 feet (246 m) above <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>. The lowest point is sea level at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast" title="Gulf Coast">Gulf Coast</a>. The state's mean elevation is 300 feet (91 m) above sea level.
</p><p>Most of Mississippi is part of the East Gulf Coastal Plain. The <a href="/wiki/Coastal_plain" title="Coastal plain">coastal plain</a> is generally composed of low hills, such as the Pine Hills in the south and the North Central Hills. The Pontotoc Ridge and the Fall Line Hills in the northeast have somewhat higher elevations. Yellow-brown <a href="/wiki/Loess" title="Loess">loess</a> soil is found in the western parts of the state. The northeast is a region of fertile black earth that extends into the <a href="/wiki/Black_Belt_(region_of_Alabama)" title="Black Belt (region of Alabama)">Alabama Black Belt</a>.
</p><p>The coastline includes large bays at <a href="/wiki/Bay_St._Louis,_Mississippi" title="Bay St. Louis, Mississippi">Bay St. Louis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biloxi,_Mississippi" title="Biloxi, Mississippi">Biloxi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pascagoula,_Mississippi" title="Pascagoula, Mississippi">Pascagoula</a>. It is separated from the Gulf of Mexico proper by the shallow <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Sound" title="Mississippi Sound">Mississippi Sound</a>, which is partially sheltered by <a href="/wiki/Petit_Bois_Island_(Mississippi)" title="Petit Bois Island (Mississippi)">Petit Bois Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horn_Island_(Mississippi)" title="Horn Island (Mississippi)">Horn Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ship_Island_(Mississippi)" title="Ship Island (Mississippi)">East and West Ship Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deer_Island_(Mississippi)" title="Deer Island (Mississippi)">Deer Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Round_Island_(Mississippi)" title="Round Island (Mississippi)">Round Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cat_Island_(Mississippi)" title="Cat Island (Mississippi)">Cat Island</a>.
</p><p>The northwest remainder of the state consists of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a>, a section of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Alluvial_Plain" title="Mississippi Alluvial Plain">Mississippi Alluvial Plain</a>. The plain is narrow in the south and widens north of <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg</a>. The region has rich soil, partly made up of <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a> which had been regularly deposited by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flood_plain" title="Flood plain">flood waters</a> of the Mississippi River.
</p><p>Areas under the management of the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a> include:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brices_Cross_Roads_National_Battlefield_Site" title="Brices Cross Roads National Battlefield Site">Brices Cross Roads National Battlefield Site</a> near <a href="/wiki/Baldwyn,_Mississippi" title="Baldwyn, Mississippi">Baldwyn</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_Islands_National_Seashore" title="Gulf Islands National Seashore">Gulf Islands National Seashore</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Natchez_National_Historical_Park" title="Natchez National Historical Park">Natchez National Historical Park</a> in <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natchez_Trace_National_Scenic_Trail" title="Natchez Trace National Scenic Trail">Natchez Trace National Scenic Trail</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tupelo,_Mississippi" title="Tupelo, Mississippi">Tupelo</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Natchez_Trace_Parkway" title="Natchez Trace Parkway">Natchez Trace Parkway</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tupelo_National_Battlefield" title="Tupelo National Battlefield">Tupelo National Battlefield</a> in Tupelo</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_National_Military_Park" title="Vicksburg National Military Park">Vicksburg National Military Park and Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg</a></li></ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Montgomery_County_(Mississippi)2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Montgomery_County_%28Mississippi%292.jpg/220px-Montgomery_County_%28Mississippi%292.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Montgomery_County_%28Mississippi%292.jpg/330px-Montgomery_County_%28Mississippi%292.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Montgomery_County_%28Mississippi%292.jpg/440px-Montgomery_County_%28Mississippi%292.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Montgomery_County_(Mississippi)2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Mississippi" title="Montgomery County, Mississippi">Montgomery County</a> in autumn</div></div></div>
<p>Mississippi has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> with long, hot and humid summers, and short, mild winters. Temperatures average about 81°<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">F</a> (about 27°<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">C</a>) in July and about 48 °F (about 9 °C) in January. The temperature varies little statewide in the summer; however, in winter, the region near <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Sound" title="Mississippi Sound">Mississippi Sound</a> is significantly warmer than the inland portion of the state. The recorded temperature in Mississippi has ranged from −19 °F (−28 °C), in 1966, at <a href="/wiki/Corinth,_Mississippi" title="Corinth, Mississippi">Corinth</a> in the northeast, to 115 °F (46 °C), in 1930, at <a href="/wiki/Holly_Springs,_Mississippi" title="Holly Springs, Mississippi">Holly Springs</a> in the north. Heavy snowfall rarely occurs, but isn’t unheard of, such as during the <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Eve_1963_snowstorm" title="New Year's Eve 1963 snowstorm">New Year's Eve 1963 snowstorm</a>. Yearly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> generally increases from north to south, with the regions closer to the Gulf being the most humid. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Clarksdale,_Mississippi" title="Clarksdale, Mississippi">Clarksdale</a>, in the northwest, gets about 50 inches (about 1,270 mm) of precipitation annually and <a href="/wiki/Biloxi,_Mississippi" title="Biloxi, Mississippi">Biloxi</a>, in the south, about 61 inches (about 1,550 mm). Small amounts of snow fall in northern and central Mississippi; snow is occasional in the southern part of the state.
</p><p>The late summer and fall is the seasonal period of risk for <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">hurricanes</a> moving inland from the Gulf of Mexico, especially in the southern part of the state. <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Camille" title="Hurricane Camille">Hurricane Camille</a> in 1969 and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> in 2005, which killed 238 people in the state, were the most devastating hurricanes to hit the state. Both caused nearly total <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a> destruction of structures in and around <a href="/wiki/Gulfport,_Mississippi" title="Gulfport, Mississippi">Gulfport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biloxi,_Mississippi" title="Biloxi, Mississippi">Biloxi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pascagoula,_Mississippi" title="Pascagoula, Mississippi">Pascagoula</a>.
</p><p>As in the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a>, thunderstorms are common in Mississippi, especially in the southern part of the state. On average, Mississippi has around 27 <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a> annually; the northern part of the state has more tornadoes earlier in the year and the southern part a higher frequency later in the year. Two of the five deadliest tornadoes in U.S. history have occurred in the state. These storms struck <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez</a>, in southwest Mississippi (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Great_Natchez_Tornado" title="The Great Natchez Tornado">The Great Natchez Tornado</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Tupelo,_Mississippi" title="Tupelo, Mississippi">Tupelo</a>, in the northeast corner of the state. About seven F5 tornadoes have been recorded in the state.
</p>

<p><br/>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ecology.2C_flora.2C_and_fauna"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology,_flora,_and_fauna">Ecology, flora, and fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ecology, flora, and fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg/220px-Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg/330px-Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg/440px-Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Welcometomississippi_i-20.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Mississippi state sign located on <a href="/wiki/Interstate_20" title="Interstate 20">Interstate 20</a> West from <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4928" data-file-width="3264" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg/220px-Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg/330px-Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg/440px-Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clark_creek_natural_area_ms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Waterfall at Clark Creek Natural Area, in the deep loess region of western Wilkinson County</div></div></div>
<p>Mississippi is heavily forested, with over half of the state's area covered by wild or cultivated trees. The southeastern part of the state is dominated by <a href="/wiki/Longleaf_pine" title="Longleaf pine">longleaf pine</a>, in both uplands and lowland <a href="/wiki/Flatwoods" title="Flatwoods">flatwoods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pitcher_plant" title="Pitcher plant">Sarracenia</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bogs" title="Bogs">bogs</a>. The Mississippi Alluvial Plain, or <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Delta</a>, is primarily farmland and aquaculture ponds but also has sizeable tracts of <a href="/wiki/Populus_deltoides" title="Populus deltoides">cottonwood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salix_nigra" title="Salix nigra">willows</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baldcypress" title="Baldcypress">baldcypress</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oaks</a>. A belt of <a href="/wiki/Loess" title="Loess">loess</a> extends north to south in the western part of the state, where the Mississippi Alluvial Plain reaches the first hills; this region is characterized by rich, mesic mixed hardwood forests, with some species <a href="/wiki/Disjunct_distribution" title="Disjunct distribution">disjunct</a> from Appalachian forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Two bands of historical <a href="/wiki/Prairie" title="Prairie">prairie</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jackson_Prairie" title="Jackson Prairie">Jackson Prairie</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Belt_(U.S._Region)" title="Black Belt (U.S. Region)">Black Belt</a>, run northwest to southeast in the middle and northeastern part of the state. Although these areas have been highly degraded by conversion to agriculture, a few areas remain, consisting of grassland with interspersed woodland of <a href="/wiki/Juniperus_virginiana" title="Juniperus virginiana">eastern redcedar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oaks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">hickories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maclura_pomifera" title="Maclura pomifera">osage-orange</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Celtis" title="Celtis">sugarberry</a>. The rest of the state, primarily north of <a href="/wiki/Interstate_20" title="Interstate 20">Interstate 20</a> not including the prairie regions, consists of mixed pine-hardwood forest, common species being <a href="/wiki/Pinus_taeda" title="Pinus taeda">loblolly pine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oaks</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Quercus_nigra" title="Quercus nigra">water oak</a>), <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">hickories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquidambar_styraciflua" title="Liquidambar styraciflua">sweetgum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Elm" title="Elm">elm</a>. Areas along large rivers are commonly inhabited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baldcypress" title="Baldcypress">baldcypress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyssa_aquatica" title="Nyssa aquatica">water tupelo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planera_aquatica" title="Planera aquatica">water elm</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carya_aquatica" title="Carya aquatica">bitter pecan</a>. Commonly cultivated trees include loblolly pine, longleaf pine, <a href="/wiki/Quercus_pagoda" title="Quercus pagoda">cherrybark oak</a>, and cottonwood.
</p><p>There are approximately 3000 species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vascular_plants" title="Vascular plants">vascular plants</a> known from Mississippi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> As of 2018, a project funded by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a> aims to update that checklist of plants with museum (<a href="/wiki/Herbarium" title="Herbarium">herbarium</a>) <a href="/wiki/Plant_collecting" title="Plant collecting">vouchers</a> and create an online <a href="/wiki/Atlas" title="Atlas">atlas</a> of each species's distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>About 420 species of <a href="/wiki/List_of_birds_of_Mississippi" title="List of birds of Mississippi">birds are known to inhabit Mississippi</a>.
</p><p>Mississippi has one of the richest fish faunas in the United States, with 204 native fish species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mississippi also has a rich <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater_mussel" title="Freshwater mussel">freshwater mussel</a> fauna, with about 90 species in the primary family of native mussels (<a href="/wiki/Unionidae" title="Unionidae">Unionidae</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Several of these species were extirpated during the construction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tennessee-Tombigbee_Waterway" title="Tennessee-Tombigbee Waterway">Tennessee-Tombigbee Waterway</a>.
</p><p>Mississippi is home to 63 <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a> species, including at least 17 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic">endemic</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mississippi is home to eight <a href="/wiki/Capniidae" title="Capniidae">winter stonefly</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecological_problems">Ecological problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ecological problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flooding">Flooding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Flooding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Due to seasonal flooding, possible from December to June, the Mississippi and Yazoo rivers and their tributaries created a fertile <a href="/wiki/Floodplain" title="Floodplain">floodplain</a> in the Mississippi Delta. The river's flooding created natural levees, which planters had built higher to try to prevent flooding of land cultivated for cotton crops. Temporary workers built <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levees" title="Levees">levees</a> along the Mississippi River on top of the natural levees that formed from dirt deposited after the river flooded.
</p><p>From 1858 to 1861, the state took over levee building, accomplishing it through contractors and hired labor. In those years, planters considered their slaves too valuable to hire out for such dangerous work. Contractors hired gangs of Irish immigrant laborers to build levees and sometimes clear land. Many of the Irish were relatively recent immigrants from the famine years who were struggling to get established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, the earthwork levees averaged six feet in height, although in some areas they reached twenty feet.
</p><p>Flooding has been an integral part of Mississippi history, but clearing of the land for cultivation and to supply wood fuel for steamboats took away the absorption of trees and undergrowth. The banks of the river were denuded, becoming unstable and changing the character of the river. After the Civil War, major floods swept down the valley in 1865, 1867, 1874 and 1882. Such floods regularly overwhelmed levees damaged by Confederate and Union fighting during the war, as well as those constructed after the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Otto_Solomon_1999,_pp.10-11_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Otto_Solomon_1999,_pp.10-11-23">[23]</a></sup> In 1877, the state created the Mississippi Levee District for southern counties.
</p><p>In 1879, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> created the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mississippi_River_Commission" title="Mississippi River Commission">Mississippi River Commission</a>, whose responsibilities included aiding state levee boards in the construction of levees. Both white and black transient workers were hired to build the levees in the late 19th century. By 1882, levees averaged seven feet in height, but many in the southern Delta were severely tested by the flood that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Otto_Solomon_1999,_pp.10-11_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-John_Otto_Solomon_1999,_pp.10-11-23">[23]</a></sup> After the 1882 flood, the levee system was expanded. In 1884, the Yazoo-Mississippi Delta Levee District was established to oversee levee construction and maintenance in the northern Delta counties; also included were some counties in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> which were part of the Delta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about_the_levee_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-about_the_levee-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Flooding overwhelmed northwestern Mississippi in 1912–1913, causing heavy damage to the levee districts. Regional losses and the Mississippi River Levee Association's lobbying for a flood control bill helped gain passage of national bills in 1917 and 1923 to provide federal matching funds for local levee districts, on a scale of 2:1. Although U.S. participation in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> interrupted funding of levees, the second round of funding helped raise the average height of levees in the Mississippi-Yazoo Delta to 22 feet (6.7 m) in the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Scientists now understand the levees have increased the severity of flooding by increasing the flow speed of the river and reducing the area of the floodplains. The region was severely damaged due to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a>, which broke through the levees. There were losses of millions of dollars in property, stock and crops. The most damage occurred in the lower Delta, including <a href="/wiki/Washington_County,_Mississippi" title="Washington County, Mississippi">Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bolivar_County,_Mississippi" title="Bolivar County, Mississippi">Bolivar</a> counties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even as scientific knowledge about the Mississippi River has grown, upstream development and the consequences of the levees have caused more severe flooding in some years. Scientists now understand that the widespread clearing of land and building of the levees have changed the nature of the river. Such work removed the natural protection and absorption of wetlands and forest cover, strengthening the river's current. The state and federal governments have been struggling for the best approaches to restore some natural habitats in order to best interact with the original riverine ecology.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
