<a href="/wiki/Lists_of_United_States_state_symbols" title="Lists of United States state symbols">Lists of United States state symbols</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Near 10,000 BC <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> or <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Paleo-Indians</a> arrived in what today is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">American South</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guy_prentice_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-guy_prentice-27">[27]</a></sup> Paleoindians in the South were <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> who pursued the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene_megafauna" title="Pleistocene megafauna">megafauna</a> that became extinct following the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> age. In the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a>, Native American settlements and agricultural fields were developed on the natural levees, higher ground in the proximity of rivers. The Native Americans developed extensive fields near their permanent villages. Together with other practices, they created some localized deforestation but did not alter the ecology of the Mississippi Delta as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bio_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-bio-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>After thousands of years, succeeding cultures of the <a href="/wiki/Woodland_period" title="Woodland period">Woodland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a> eras developed rich and complex agricultural societies, in which surplus supported the development of specialized trades. Both were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mound_builder_(people)" title="Mound builder (people)">mound builder</a> cultures. Those of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippian_culture" title="Mississippian culture">Mississippian culture</a> were the largest and most complex, constructed beginning about 950CE. The peoples had a trading network spanning the continent from the Great Lakes to the Gulf Coast. Their large earthworks, which expressed their cosmology of political and religious concepts, still stand throughout the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mississippi_River_Valley" title="Mississippi River Valley">Mississippi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ohio_River_Valley" title="Ohio River Valley">Ohio River</a> valleys.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1026" data-file-width="1460" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg/220px-Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg/330px-Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg/440px-Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Choctaw_Village_by_Francois_Bernard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Choctaw Village near the Chefuncte</i>, by Francois Bernard, 1869, Peabody Museum – Harvard University. The women are preparing dye in order to color cane strips for making baskets.</div></div></div>
<p>Descendant <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> tribes of the Mississippian culture in the Southeast include the <a href="/wiki/Chickasaw" title="Chickasaw">Chickasaw</a> and <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>. Other tribes who inhabited the territory of Mississippi (and whose names were honored by colonists in local towns) include the <a href="/wiki/Natchez_people" title="Natchez people">Natchez</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yazoo_tribe" title="Yazoo tribe">Yazoo</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Biloxi_people" title="Biloxi people">Biloxi</a>.
</p><p>The first major European expedition into the territory that became Mississippi was that of the Spanish explorer, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hernando_de_Soto_(explorer)" title="Hernando de Soto (explorer)">Hernando de Soto</a>, who passed through the northeast part of the state in 1540, in his second expedition to the New World.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_era">Colonial era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Colonial era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In April 1699, French colonists established the first European settlement at <i><a href="/wiki/Fort_Maurepas" title="Fort Maurepas">Fort Maurepas</a></i> (also known as Old Biloxi), built in the vicinity of present-day <a href="/wiki/Ocean_Springs,_Mississippi" title="Ocean Springs, Mississippi">Ocean Springs</a> on the Gulf Coast. It was settled by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Le_Moyne_d%27Iberville" title="Pierre Le Moyne d'Iberville">Pierre Le Moyne d'Iberville</a>. In 1716, the French founded <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez</a> on the Mississippi River (as <i><a href="/wiki/Fort_Rosalie" title="Fort Rosalie">Fort Rosalie</a></i>); it became the dominant town and trading post of the area. The French called the greater territory "<a href="/wiki/Louisiana_(New_France)" title="Louisiana (New France)">New France</a>"; the Spanish continued to claim part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_coast" title="Gulf coast">Gulf coast</a> area (east of <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Bay" title="Mobile Bay">Mobile Bay</a>) of present-day southern <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, in addition to the entire area of present-day <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1266" data-file-width="1119" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg/170px-Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg/255px-Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg/340px-Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pushmataha_high_resolution.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pushmataha" title="Pushmataha">Pushmataha</a>, Choctaw Principal Chief</div></div></div><p> Through the 18th century, the area was ruled variously by Spanish, French, and British colonial governments. The colonists imported African <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">slaves</a> as laborers. Under French and Spanish rule, there developed a class of <a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">free people of color</a> (<i>gens de couleur libres</i>), mostly <a href="/wiki/Multiracial" title="Multiracial">multiracial</a> descendants of European men and enslaved or free black women, and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed-race" title="Mixed-race">mixed-race</a> children. In the early days the French and Spanish colonists were chiefly men. Even as more European women joined the settlements, the men had interracial unions among women of African descent (and increasingly, multiracial descent), both before and after marriages to European women. Often the European men would help their multiracial children get educated or gain apprenticeships for trades, and sometimes they settled property on them; they often freed the mothers and their children if enslaved, as part of contracts of <i><a href="/wiki/Pla%C3%A7age" title="Plaçage">plaçage</a>.</i> With this <a href="/wiki/Social_capital" title="Social capital">social capital</a>, the free people of color became artisans, and sometimes educated merchants and property owners, forming a third class between the Europeans and most enslaved Africans in the French and Spanish settlements, although not so large a free community as in the city of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>, Louisiana. 
</p><p>After Great Britain's victory in the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a> (<a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>), the French surrendered the Mississippi area to them under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1763)" title="Treaty of Paris (1763)">Treaty of Paris (1763)</a>. They also ceded their areas to the north that were east of the Mississippi River, including the Illinois Country and Quebec. After the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Peace of Paris (1783)">Peace of Paris (1783)</a>, the lower third of Mississippi came under Spanish rule as part of <a href="/wiki/West_Florida" title="West Florida">West Florida</a>. In 1819 the United States completed the purchase of West Florida and all of <a href="/wiki/East_Florida" title="East Florida">East Florida</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">Adams–Onís Treaty</a>, and in 1822 both were merged into the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Territory" title="Florida Territory">Florida Territory</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_territory">United States territory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: United States territory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Territory" title="Mississippi Territory">Mississippi Territory</a></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> (1765–83), Britain ceded this area to the new United States of America. The <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Territory" title="Mississippi Territory">Mississippi Territory</a> was <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">organized</a> on April 7, 1798, from territory ceded by <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a> to the United States. Their original colonial charters theoretically extended west to the Pacific Ocean. The Mississippi Territory was later twice expanded to include disputed territory claimed by both the United States and Spain.
</p><p>From 1800 to about 1830, the United States purchased some lands (<a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Doak%27s_Stand" title="Treaty of Doak's Stand">Treaty of Doak's Stand</a>) from Native American tribes for new settlements of European Americans; they were mostly migrants from other Southern states, particularly Virginia and North Carolina, where soils were exhausted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> On September 27, 1830, the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Dancing_Rabbit_Creek" title="Treaty of Dancing Rabbit Creek">Treaty of Dancing Rabbit Creek</a> was signed between the U.S. Government and the <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>. The Choctaw agreed to sell their traditional homelands in Mississippi and Alabama, for compensation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_Removal" title="Indian Removal">removal</a> to reservations in <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">Indian Territory</a> (now Oklahoma). This opened up land for sale to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European-American" title="European-American">European-American</a> immigrant settlement. Article 14 in the treaty allowed those Choctaw who chose to remain in the state to become U.S. citizens, as they were considered to be giving up their tribal membership. They were the second major non-European ethnic group to do so (the Cherokee were the first).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_citizenship_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-us_citizenship-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-david_baird_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-david_baird-31">[31]</a></sup> Today approximately 9,500 Choctaw live in Neshoba, Newton, Leake, and Jones counties. Federally recognized tribes include the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Band_of_Choctaw_Indians" title="Mississippi Band of Choctaw Indians">Mississippi Band of Choctaw Indians</a>.
</p><p>Many slaveholders brought slaves with them or purchased them through the domestic slave trade, especially in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. Through the trade, an estimated nearly one million slaves were transported to the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a>, including Mississippi, in a forced internal migration that broke up many slave families of the Upper South, where planters were selling excess slaves. The Southerners imposed slave laws and restricted the rights of free blacks, according to their view of <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">white supremacy</a>.
</p><p>Beginning in 1822, slaves in Mississippi were protected by law from cruel and unusual punishment by their owners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The Southern slave codes made the willful killing of a slave illegal in most cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> For example, the 1860 Mississippi case of <i>Oliver v. State</i> charged the defendant with murdering his own slave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D%27Evereux.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3262" data-file-width="4641" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/D%27Evereux.jpg/220px-D%27Evereux.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/D%27Evereux.jpg/330px-D%27Evereux.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/D%27Evereux.jpg/440px-D%27Evereux.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D%27Evereux.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Big House at D'Evereux Plantation. Built in 1840, the mansion is listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places_listings_in_Mississippi" title="National Register of Historic Places listings in Mississippi">National Register of Historic Places</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Statehood.2C_1817.E2.80.931861"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Statehood,_1817–1861">Statehood, 1817–1861</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Statehood, 1817–1861">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On December 10, 1817, Mississippi was the 20th state admitted to the Union. <a href="/wiki/David_Holmes_(politician)" title="David Holmes (politician)">David Holmes</a> was elected as the first governor of the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> At that time, the state was still occupied as ancestral land by several Native American tribes, including the Choctaw, Natchez, Houma, Creek, and Chickasaw peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plantations were developed primarily along the major rivers, where the waterfront provided access to the major transportation routes. This is also where early towns developed,linked by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steamboats" title="Steamboats">steamboats</a> that carried commercial products and crops to markets. The remainder of Native American ancestral land remained largely undeveloped but was sold through treaties until 1826, when the Choctaws and Chickasaws refused to sell more land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> 
The combination of the Mississippi state legislature's abolition of Choctaw Tribal Government in 1829,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> President Andrew Jackson's <a href="/wiki/Indian_Removal_Act" title="Indian Removal Act">Indian Removal Act</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Dancing_Rabbit_Creek" title="Treaty of Dancing Rabbit Creek">Treaty of Dancing Rabbit Creek</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> of 1830, the Choctaw were effectively forced to sell their land and were transported to Oklahoma Territory. The forced migration of the Choctaw, together with other southeastern tribes removed as a result of the Act, became known as the <a href="/wiki/Trail_of_Tears" title="Trail of Tears">Trail of Tears</a>.
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/King_Cotton" title="King Cotton">cotton was king</a> during the 1850s, Mississippi plantation owners—especially those of the Delta and Black Belt central regions—became wealthy due to the high fertility of the soil, the high price of cotton on the international market, and free labor gained through their holding enslaved African Americans. They used some of their profits to buy more cotton land and more slaves. The planters' dependence on hundreds of thousands of slaves for labor and the severe wealth imbalances among whites, played strong roles both in state politics and in planters' support for <a href="/wiki/Secession" title="Secession">secession</a>. Mississippi was a slave society, with the economy dependent on slavery. The state was thinly settled, with population concentrated in the riverfront areas and towns.
</p><p>By 1860, the enslaved African-American population numbered 436,631 or 55% of the state's total of 791,305 persons. Fewer than 1000 were <a href="/wiki/Free_people_of_color" title="Free people of color">free people of color</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The relatively low population of the state before the Civil War reflected the fact that land and villages were developed only along the riverfronts, which formed the main transportation corridors. Ninety percent of the Delta bottomlands were still frontier and undeveloped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup> The state needed many more settlers for development. The land further away from the rivers was cleared by freedmen and white migrants during Reconstruction and later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War_to_20th_century">Civil War to 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Civil War to 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1038" data-file-width="1200" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg/220px-ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg/330px-ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg/440px-ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ConfederateDeadBatteryRobinettCorinth1862.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Confederate dead after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Battle_of_Corinth" title="Second Battle of Corinth">Battle of Corinth</a>. Photo taken October 5, 1862</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_(1890).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="658" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_%281890%29.png/220px-Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_%281890%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_%281890%29.png/330px-Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_%281890%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_%281890%29.png/440px-Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_%281890%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Legislature_of_the_State_of_Mississippi_(1890).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The legislature of the State of Mississippi in 1890</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Mississippi in the American Civil War">Mississippi in the American Civil War</a></div>
<p>On January 9, 1861, Mississippi became the second state to declare its secession from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_(Civil_War)" title="Union (Civil War)">Union</a>, and it was one of the founding members of the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States</a>. The first six states to secede were those with the highest number of slaves. During the war, Union and Confederate forces struggled for dominance on the Mississippi River, critical to supply routes and commerce. More than 80,000 Mississippians fought in the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Mississippi in the American Civil War">Civil War</a>, and casualties were extremely heavy. Union General <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>'s long siege of <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Campaign" title="Vicksburg Campaign">Vicksburg</a> finally gained the Union control of the river in 1863.
</p><p>In the postwar period, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedmen" title="Freedmen">freedmen</a> withdrew from white-run churches to set up independent congregations. The majority of blacks left the Southern Baptist Church, sharply reducing its membership. They created independent black Baptist congregations. By 1895 they had established numerous black Baptist state associations and the <a href="/wiki/National_Baptist_Convention,_USA,_Inc." title="National Baptist Convention, USA, Inc.">National Baptist Convention</a> of black churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, independent black denominations, such as the <a href="/wiki/African_Methodist_Episcopal_Church" title="African Methodist Episcopal Church">African Methodist Episcopal Church</a> (established in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" title="Philadelphia, Pennsylvania">Philadelphia, Pennsylvania</a> in the early 19th century) and the <a href="/wiki/African_Methodist_Episcopal_Zion_Church" title="African Methodist Episcopal Zion Church">African Methodist Episcopal Zion Church</a> (established in New York City), sent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missionaries" title="Missionaries">missionaries</a> to the South in the postwar years. They quickly attracted hundreds of thousands of converts and founded new churches across the South. Southern congregations brought their own influences to those denominations as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-docsouth.unc.edu_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-docsouth.unc.edu-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction era of the United States">Reconstruction</a>, the first Mississippi constitutional convention in 1868, with delegates both black and white, framed a constitution whose major elements would be maintained for 22 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dubois_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-dubois-45">[45]</a></sup> The convention was the first political organization in the state to include African-American representatives, 17 among the 100 members (32 counties had black majorities at the time). Some among the black delegates were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freedmen" title="Freedmen">freedmen</a>, but others were educated free blacks who had migrated from the North. The convention adopted universal suffrage; did away with property qualifications for <a href="/wiki/Suffrage" title="Suffrage">suffrage</a> or for office, a change that also benefited both blacks and poor whites; provided for the state's first public school system; forbade race distinctions in the possession and inheritance of property; and prohibited limiting civil rights in travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dubois_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-dubois-45">[45]</a></sup> Under the terms of Reconstruction, Mississippi was restored to the Union on February 23, 1870.
</p><p>Because the Mississippi Delta contained so much fertile bottomland that had not been developed before the Civil War, 90 percent of the land was still frontier. After the Civil War, tens of thousands of migrants were attracted to the area by higher wages offered by planters trying to develop land. In addition, black and white workers could earn money by clearing the land and selling timber, and eventually advance to ownership. The new farmers included many freedmen, who by the late 19th century achieved unusually high rates of land ownership in the Mississippi bottomlands. In the 1870s and 1880s, many black farmers succeeded in gaining land ownership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the start of the 20th century, two-thirds of the Mississippi farmers who owned land in the Delta were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-3"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup> But many had become overextended with debt during the falling cotton prices of the difficult years of the late 19th century. Cotton prices fell throughout the decades following the Civil War. As another agricultural depression lowered cotton prices into the 1890s, numerous African-American farmers finally had to sell their land to pay off debts, thus losing the land which they had developed by hard, personal labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-4"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Democrats had regained control of the state legislature in 1875, after a year of expanded violence against blacks and intimidation of whites in what was called the "white line" campaign, based on asserting <a href="/wiki/White_supremacy" title="White supremacy">white supremacy</a>. Democratic whites were well armed and formed <a href="/wiki/Paramilitary" title="Paramilitary">paramilitary</a> organizations such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Shirts_(southern_United_States)" title="Red Shirts (southern United States)">Red Shirts</a> to suppress black voting. From 1874 to the elections of 1875, they pressured whites to join the Democrats, and conducted violence against blacks in at least 15 known "riots" in cities around the state to intimidate blacks. They killed a total of 150 blacks, although other estimates place the death toll at twice as many. A total of three white Republicans and five white Democrats were reported killed. In rural areas, deaths of blacks could be covered up. Riots (better described as massacres of blacks) took place in Vicksburg, Clinton, Macon, and in their counties, as well-armed whites broke up black meetings and <a href="/wiki/Lynching" title="Lynching">lynched</a> known black leaders, destroying local political organizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Seeing the success of this deliberate "<a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Plan" title="Mississippi Plan">Mississippi Plan</a>", South Carolina and other states followed it and also achieved white Democratic dominance. In 1877 by a national compromise, the last of federal troops were withdrawn from the region.
</p><p>Even in this environment, black Mississippians continued to be elected to local office. However, black residents were deprived of all political power after white legislators passed a new state constitution in 1890 specifically to "eliminate the nigger from politics", according to the state's <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> governor, <a href="/wiki/James_K._Vardaman" title="James K. Vardaman">James K. Vardaman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NRMcMillenP43_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-NRMcMillenP43-47">[47]</a></sup> It erected barriers to voter registration and instituted electoral provisions that effectively <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after Reconstruction era">disenfranchised</a> most black Mississippians and many poor whites. Estimates are that 100,000 black and 50,000 white men were removed from voter registration rolls in the state over the next few years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The loss of political influence contributed to the difficulties of African Americans in their attempts to obtain extended credit in the late 19th century. Together with imposition of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a> and racial segregation laws, whites increased violence against blacks, lynching mostly men, through the period of the 1890s and extending to 1930. Cotton crops failed due to <a href="/wiki/Boll_weevil" title="Boll weevil">boll weevil</a> infestation and successive severe flooding in 1912 and 1913, creating crisis conditions for many African Americans. With control of the ballot box and more access to credit, white planters bought out such farmers, expanding their ownership of Delta bottomlands. They also took advantage of new railroads sponsored by the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-5"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:All_these_children.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="685" data-file-width="993" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/All_these_children.jpg/220px-All_these_children.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/All_these_children.jpg/330px-All_these_children.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/All_these_children.jpg/440px-All_these_children.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:All_these_children.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Child workers, <a href="/wiki/Pass_Christian,_Mississippi" title="Pass Christian, Mississippi">Pass Christian</a>, 1911, by <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Hine" title="Lewis Hine">Lewis Hine</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_to_present">20th century to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 20th century to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1900, blacks made up more than half of the state's population. By 1910, a majority of black farmers in the Delta had lost their land and become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharecropper" title="Sharecropper">sharecroppers</a>. By 1920, the third generation after freedom, most African Americans in Mississippi were landless laborers again facing poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-6"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup> Starting about 1913, tens of thousands of black Americans left Mississippi for the <a href="/wiki/Northern_United_States" title="Northern United States">North</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">Great Migration</a> to industrial cities such as <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cleveland" title="Cleveland">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a>. They sought jobs, better education for their children, the right to vote, relative freedom from discrimination, and better living. In the migration of 1910–1940, they left a society that had been steadily closing off opportunity. Most migrants from Mississippi took trains directly north to Chicago and often settled near former neighbors.
</p><p>Blacks also faced violence in the form of lynching, shooting, and the burning of churches. In 1923, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Colored_People" title="National Association for the Advancement of Colored People">National Association for the Advancement of Colored People</a> stated "the Negro feels that life is not safe in Mississippi and his life may be taken with impunity at any time upon the slightest pretext or provocation by a white man".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="417" data-file-width="560" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg/220px-FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg/330px-FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg/440px-FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FSA_Dancing_JukeJoint.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dancing at a <a href="/wiki/Juke_joint" title="Juke joint">juke joint</a> near Clarksdale, Mississippi, in 1939, by <a href="/wiki/Marion_Post_Wolcott" title="Marion Post Wolcott">Marion Post Wolcott</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the early 20th century, some industries were established in Mississippi, but jobs were generally restricted to whites, including child workers. The lack of jobs also drove some southern whites north to cities such as Chicago and Detroit, seeking employment, where they also competed with European immigrants. The state depended on agriculture, but mechanization put many farm laborers out of work.
</p><p>By 1900, many white ministers, especially in the towns, subscribed to the <a href="/wiki/Social_Gospel" title="Social Gospel">Social Gospel</a> movement, which attempted to apply Christian ethics to social and economic needs of the day. Many strongly supported <a href="/wiki/Prohibition" title="Prohibition">Prohibition</a>, believing it would help alleviate and prevent many sins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>African-American Baptist churches grew to include more than twice the number of members as their white Baptist counterparts. The African-American call for social equality resonated throughout the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> in the 1930s and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in the 1940s.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Second_Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Second Great Migration (African American)">Second Great Migration</a> from the South started in the 1940s, lasting until 1970. Almost half a million people left Mississippi in the second migration, three-quarters of them black. Nationwide during the first half of the 20th century, African Americans became rapidly urbanized and many worked in industrial jobs. The Second Great Migration included destinations in the <a href="/wiki/Western_United_States" title="Western United States">West</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, where the buildup of the defense industry offered higher paying jobs to both African Americans and whites.
</p><p>Blacks and whites in Mississippi generated rich, quintessentially American music traditions: <a href="/wiki/Gospel_music" title="Gospel music">gospel music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Country_music" title="Country music">country music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">rock and roll</a>. All were invented, promulgated or heavily developed by Mississippi musicians, many of them African American, and most came from the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a>. Many musicians carried their music north to Chicago, where they made it the heart of that city's jazz and blues.
</p><p>So many African Americans left in the Great Migration that after the 1930s, they became a minority in Mississippi. In 1960 they made up 42% of the state's population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The whites maintained their discriminatory voter registration processes established in 1890, preventing most blacks from voting, even if they were well educated. Court challenges were not successful until later in the century. After World War II, African-American veterans returned with renewed commitment to be treated as full citizens of the United States and increasingly organized to gain enforcement of their constitutional rights.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">Civil Rights Movement</a> had many roots in religion, and the strong community of churches helped supply volunteers and moral purpose for their activism. Mississippi was a center of activity, based in black churches, to educate and register black voters, and to work for integration. In 1954 the state had created the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_State_Sovereignty_Commission" title="Mississippi State Sovereignty Commission">Mississippi State Sovereignty Commission</a>, a tax-supported agency, chaired by the Governor, that claimed to work for the state's image but effectively spied on activists and passed information to the local White Citizens' Councils to suppress black activism. <a href="/wiki/Citizens%27_Councils" title="Citizens' Councils">White Citizens Councils</a> had been formed in many cities and towns to resist integration of schools following the unanimous 1954 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> ruling (<i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a></i>) that segregation of public schools was unconstitutional. They used intimidation and economic blackmail against activists and suspected activists, including teachers and other professionals. Techniques included loss of jobs and eviction from rental housing.
</p><p>In the summer of 1964 students and community organizers from across the country came to help register black voters in Mississippi and establish <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Schools" title="Freedom Schools">Freedom Schools</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Freedom_Democratic_Party" title="Mississippi Freedom Democratic Party">Mississippi Freedom Democratic Party</a> was established to challenge the all-white Democratic Party of the <a href="/wiki/Solid_South" title="Solid South">Solid South</a>. Most white politicians resisted such changes. Chapters of the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> and its sympathizers used violence against activists, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Murders_of_Chaney,_Goodman,_and_Schwerner" title="Murders of Chaney, Goodman, and Schwerner">murders of Chaney, Goodman, and Schwerner</a> in 1964 during the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Summer" title="Freedom Summer">Freedom Summer</a> campaign. This was a catalyst for Congressional passage the following year of the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a>. Mississippi earned a reputation in the 1960s as a reactionary state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>After decades of disenfranchisement, African Americans in the state gradually began to exercise their right to vote again for the first time since the 19th century, following the passage of federal civil rights legislation in 1964 and 1965, which ended <i>de jure</i> segregation and enforced constitutional voting rights. Registration of African-American voters increased and black candidates ran in the 1967 elections for state and local offices. The Mississippi Freedom Democratic Party fielded some candidates. Teacher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_G._Clark,_Jr." title="Robert G. Clark, Jr.">Robert G. Clark</a> of Holmes County was the first African American to be elected to the State House since Reconstruction. He continued as the only African American in the state legislature until 1976 and was repeatedly elected into the 21st century, including three terms as Speaker of the House.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-msu_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-msu-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1966, the state was the last to repeal officially statewide <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">prohibition of alcohol</a>. Before that, Mississippi had taxed the illegal alcohol brought in by <a href="/wiki/Rum-running" title="Rum-running">bootleggers</a>. Governor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paul_B._Johnson,_Jr." title="Paul B. Johnson, Jr.">Paul Johnson</a> urged repeal and the sheriff "raided the annual <a href="/wiki/Junior_League" title="Junior League">Junior League</a> <a href="/wiki/Mardi_Gras" title="Mardi Gras">Mardi Gras</a> ball at the Jackson Country Club, breaking open the liquor cabinet and carting off the Champagne before a startled crowd of <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> and high-ranking state officials".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>On August 17, 1969, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir-Simpson_Hurricane_Scale" title="Saffir-Simpson Hurricane Scale">Category 5</a> <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Camille" title="Hurricane Camille">Hurricane Camille</a> hit the Mississippi coast, killing 248 people and causing US$1.5 billion in damage (1969 dollars).
</p><p>Mississippi was the last state to ratify the <a href="/wiki/Nineteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Nineteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Nineteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution</a>, in March 1984, granting women the right to vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1987, 20 years after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" title="United States Supreme Court">U.S. Supreme Court</a> had ruled in 1967's <i><a href="/wiki/Loving_v._Virginia" title="Loving v. Virginia">Loving v. Virginia</a></i> that a similar Virginian law was unconstitutional, Mississippi repealed its ban on interracial marriage (also known as <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a>), which had been enacted in 1890. It also repealed the <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">segregationist</a>-era <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(United_States)" title="Poll tax (United States)">poll tax</a> in 1989. In 1995, the state symbolically ratified the <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Thirteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Thirteenth Amendment</a>, which had abolished slavery in 1865. Though ratified in 1995, the state never officially notified the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Archivist" title="Archivist">archivist</a>, which kept the ratification unofficial until 2013, when Ken Sullivan contacted the office of <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_of_Mississippi" title="Secretary of State of Mississippi">Secretary of State of Mississippi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delbert_Hosemann" title="Delbert Hosemann">Delbert Hosemann</a>, who agreed to file the paperwork and make it official.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In 2009, the legislature passed a bill to repeal other discriminatory civil rights laws, which had been enacted in 1964, the same year as the federal <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act</a>, but ruled unconstitutional in 1967 by federal courts. Republican Governor <a href="/wiki/Haley_Barbour" title="Haley Barbour">Haley Barbour</a> signed the bill into law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The end of legal segregation and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jim_Crow" title="Jim Crow">Jim Crow</a> led to the integration of some churches, but most today remain divided along racial and cultural lines, having developed different traditions. After the Civil War, most African Americans left white churches to establish their own independent congregations, particularly Baptist churches, establishing state associations and a national association by the end of the century. They wanted to express their own traditions of worship and practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In more diverse communities, such as <a href="/wiki/Hattiesburg,_Mississippi" title="Hattiesburg, Mississippi">Hattiesburg</a>, some churches have multiracial congregations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTrace_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTrace-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg/220px-NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg/330px-NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg/440px-NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NOAA-Hurricane-Katrina-Aug28-05-2145UTC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hurricane Katrina approaching the Gulf Coast on August 28, 2005.</div></div></div>
<p>On August 29, 2005, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>, though a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir-Simpson_Hurricane_Scale" title="Saffir-Simpson Hurricane Scale">Category 3</a> storm upon final landfall, caused even greater destruction across the entire 90 miles (145 km) of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Gulf_Coast" title="Mississippi Gulf Coast">Mississippi Gulf Coast</a> from Louisiana to Alabama.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</tbody></table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Center_of_population" title="Center of population">center of population</a> of Mississippi is located in <a href="/wiki/Leake_County,_Mississippi" title="Leake County, Mississippi">Leake County</a>, in the town of <a href="/wiki/Lena,_Mississippi" title="Lena, Mississippi">Lena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_population_map_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="610" data-file-width="560" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mississippi_population_map_2.png/220px-Mississippi_population_map_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mississippi_population_map_2.png/330px-Mississippi_population_map_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Mississippi_population_map_2.png/440px-Mississippi_population_map_2.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_population_map_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mississippi population density map</div></div></div><p> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> estimates that the population of Mississippi was 2,992,333 on July 1, 2015, a 0.84% increase since the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">2010 United States Census</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PopEstUS_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-PopEstUS-64">[64]</a></sup> The state's economist characterized the state as losing population as job markets elsewhere have caused 3.2 per 1000 to migrate recently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2000 to 2010, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> reported that Mississippi had the highest rate of increase in people identifying as mixed-race, up 70 percent in the decade; it amounts to a total of 1.1 percent of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTrace_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTrace-62">[62]</a></sup> In addition, Mississippi led the nation for most of the last decade in the growth of mixed marriages among its population. The total population has not increased significantly, but is young. Some of the above change in identification as mixed race is due to new births. But, it appears mostly to reflect those residents who have chosen to identify as more than one race, who in earlier years may have identified by just one ethnicity. A binary racial system had been in place since slavery times and the days of <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a>. In the civil rights era, people of African descent banded together in an inclusive community to achieve political power and gain restoration of their civil rights.
</p><p>As the demographer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Frey" title="William Frey">William Frey</a> noted, "In Mississippi, I think it's [identifying as mixed race] changed from within."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTrace_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-NYTrace-62">[62]</a></sup> Historically in Mississippi, after <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">Indian removal</a> in the 1830s, the major groups were designated as black (African American), who were then mostly enslaved, and white (primarily European American). <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Matthew_Snipp&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Matthew Snipp (page does not exist)">Matthew Snipp</a>, also a demographer, commented on the increase in the 21st century in the number of people identifying as being of more than one race: "In a sense, they're rendering a more accurate portrait of their racial heritage that in the past would have been suppressed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTrace_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-NYTrace-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>After having comprised a majority of the state's population since well before the Civil War and through the 1930s, today <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African Americans</a> comprise approximately 37 percent of the state's population. Most have ancestors who were <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">enslaved</a>, with many forcibly transported from the Upper South in the 19th century to work on the area's new plantations. Some of these slaves were mixed race, with European ancestors, as there were many children born into slavery with white fathers. Some also have Native American ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> During the first half of the 20th century, a total of nearly 400,000 African Americans left the state during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">Great Migration</a>, for opportunities in the North, Midwest and West. They became a minority in the state for the first time since early in its development.
</p><p>The state has had conservative laws related to sexuality. The state's <a href="/wiki/Sodomy_laws_in_the_United_States" title="Sodomy laws in the United States">sodomy law</a> criminalized consensual sex between adults of the same gender until 2003 (but was seldom enforced), when such laws were voided by the Supreme Court case <i><a href="/wiki/Lawrence_v._Texas" title="Lawrence v. Texas">Lawrence v. Texas</a></i>. In 2004, voters in Mississippi approved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mississippi_Amendment_1_(2004)" title="Mississippi Amendment 1 (2004)">Amendment 1</a>, <a href="/wiki/U.S._state_constitutional_amendments_banning_same-sex_unions" title="U.S. state constitutional amendments banning same-sex unions">amending</a> the state's <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Mississippi" title="Constitution of Mississippi">constitution</a> to prohibit <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_United_States" title="Same-sex marriage in the United States">same-sex marriage</a>; the measure passed with 86% of the vote, the highest margin of victory in the nation. This law was overturned by <i><a href="/wiki/Obergefell_v._Hodges" title="Obergefell v. Hodges">Obergefell v. Hodges</a></i> (2015), the decision of the U.S. Supreme Court making same-sex marriage a constitutional right.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite conservative laws, same-sex couples were forming families in the state. According to the <a href="/wiki/2010_United_States_Census" title="2010 United States Census">2010 census</a>, approximately 33% of households led by same-sex couples in Mississippi included at least one child, the highest such percentage in the nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestry">Ancestry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ancestry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At the 2010 U.S. census, the racial makeup of the population was:
</p>
<ul><li>59.1% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_American" title="White American">White American</a> (58.0% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Hispanic_white" title="Non-Hispanic white">non-Hispanic white</a>, 1.1% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Hispanic" title="White Hispanic">White Hispanic</a>)</li>
<li>37.0% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> or Black</li>
<li>0.5% <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">American Indian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_Native" title="Alaska Native">Alaska Native</a></li>
<li>0.9% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_American" title="Asian American">Asian American</a></li>
<li>1.1% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiracial_American" title="Multiracial American">Multiracial American</a></li>
<li>1.4% Other</li></ul>
<p>Ethnically, 2.7% of the total population, among all racial groups, was of Hispanic or Latino origin (they may be of any race).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> As of 2011, 53.8% of Mississippi's population younger than age 1 were minorities, meaning that they had at least one parent who was not non-Hispanic white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> For more information on racial and ethnic classifications in the United States see <a href="/wiki/Race_and_ethnicity_in_the_United_States_Census" title="Race and ethnicity in the United States Census">race and ethnicity in the United States Census</a>.
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable collapsible">

<p>Americans of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scots-Irish_American" title="Scots-Irish American">Scots-Irish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">English</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_American" title="Scottish American">Scottish</a> ancestry are present throughout the state. It is believed that there are more people with such ancestry than identify as such on the census, in part because their immigrant ancestors are more distant in their family histories. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">English</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_American" title="Scottish American">Scottish</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scots-Irish_American" title="Scots-Irish American">Scots-Irish</a> are generally the most under-reported ancestry groups in both the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_States" title="South Atlantic States">South Atlantic States</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_South_Central_States" title="East South Central States">East South Central States</a>. The historian <a href="/wiki/David_Hackett_Fischer" title="David Hackett Fischer">David Hackett Fischer</a> estimated that a minimum 20% of Mississippi's population is of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">English</a> ancestry, though the figure is probably much higher, and another large percentage is of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_American" title="Scottish American">Scottish</a> ancestry. Many Mississippians of such ancestry identify simply as American on questionnaires, because their families have been in North America for centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In the 1980 census 656,371 Mississippians of a total of 1,946,775 identified as being of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_American" title="English American">English</a> ancestry, making them 38% of the state at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The state in 2010 had the highest proportion of African Americans in the nation. The African-American percentage of population has begun to increase due mainly to a younger population than the whites (the <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rates</a> of the two races are approximately equal). Due to patterns of settlement and whites putting their children in private schools, in almost all of Mississippi's public school districts, a majority of students are African American. African Americans are the majority ethnic group in the northwestern <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Yazoo Delta</a>, and the southwestern and the central parts of the state. These are areas where, historically, African Americans owned land as farmers in the 19th century following the Civil War, or worked on cotton plantations and farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>People of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_American" title="French American">French Creole</a> ancestry form the largest demographic group in <a href="/wiki/Hancock_County,_Mississippi" title="Hancock County, Mississippi">Hancock County</a> on the Gulf Coast. The African-American; <a href="/wiki/Choctaw" title="Choctaw">Choctaw</a>, mostly in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neshoba_County" title="Neshoba County">Neshoba County</a>; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_American" title="Chinese American">Chinese-American</a> portions of the population are also almost entirely native born.
</p><p>Chinese came to Mississippi as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indentured" title="Indentured">indentured</a> laborers from <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> during the 1870s, with others coming from mainland China in the later 19th century. The majority entering the state immigrated directly from China to Mississippi between 1910 and 1930, when they were recruited by planters as laborers. While most first worked as sharecroppers, the Chinese worked as families to improve their lives. Many became small merchants and especially grocers in small towns throughout the Delta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In these roles, the ethnic Chinese carved out a niche in the state between black and white, where they were concentrated in the Delta. These small towns have declined since the late 20th century, and many ethnic Chinese have joined the exodus to larger cities, including Jackson. Their population in the state overall has increased in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1980s many <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_people" title="Vietnamese people">Vietnamese</a> immigrated to Mississippi and other states along the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, where they became employed in fishing-related work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2000, 96.4% of Mississippi residents five years old and older spoke only English in the home, a decrease from 97.2% in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-city-data.com_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-city-data.com-84">[84]</a></sup> English is largely <a href="/wiki/Southern_American_English" title="Southern American English">Southern American English</a>, with some South Midland speech in northern and eastern Mississippi. There is a common absence of final /r/ and the lengthening and weakening of the diphthongs /aɪ/ and /ɔɪ/ as in 'ride' and 'oil'. South Midland terms in northern Mississippi include: tow sack (burlap bag), dog irons (andirons), plum peach (clingstone peach), snake doctor (dragonfly), and stone wall (rock fence).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-city-data.com_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-city-data.com-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Under French and Spanish rule beginning in the 17th century, European colonists were mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a>. The growth of the cotton culture after 1815 brought in tens of thousands of Anglo-American settlers each year, most of whom were Protestants from Southeastern states. Due to such migration, there was rapid growth in the number of Protestant churches, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Methodist" title="Methodist">Methodist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterian" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist">Baptist</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-religion-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2809" data-file-width="4284" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg/220px-Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg/330px-Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg/440px-Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liberty_baptist_church_amite_county_ms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Liberty Baptist Church, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist" title="Southern Baptist">Southern Baptist</a> church, the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> denomination in Mississippi, in <a href="/wiki/Liberty,_Mississippi" title="Liberty, Mississippi">Liberty</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amite_County" title="Amite County">Amite County</a>), Mississippi</div></div></div>
<p>The revivals of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Awakening" title="Great Awakening">Great Awakening</a> in the late 18th and early 19th centuries initially attracted the <a href="/wiki/Plain_Folk_of_the_Old_South" title="Plain Folk of the Old South">"plain folk"</a> by reaching out to all members of society, including women and blacks. Both slaves and free blacks were welcomed into Methodist and Baptist churches. Independent black Baptist churches were established before 1800 in Virginia, Kentucky, South Carolina and Georgia, and later developed in Mississippi as well.
</p><p>In the post-Civil War years, religion became more influential as the South became known as the "<a href="/wiki/Bible_Belt" title="Bible Belt">Bible Belt</a>".
</p><p>Since the 1970s, fundamentalist conservative churches have grown rapidly, fueling Mississippi's conservative political trends among whites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-religion_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-religion-86">[86]</a></sup> In 1973 the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_America" title="Presbyterian Church in America">Presbyterian Church in America</a> attracted numerous conservative congregations. As of 2010 Mississippi remained a stronghold of the denomination, which originally was brought by Scots immigrants. The state has the highest adherence rate of the PCA in 2010, with 121 congregations and 18,500 members. It is among the few states where the PCA has higher membership than the PC(USA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
According to the <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Religion_Data_Archives" title="Association of Religion Data Archives">Association of Religion Data Archives</a> (ARDA), in 2010 the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> had 907,384 adherents and was the largest religious denomination in the state, followed by the <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">United Methodist Church</a> with 204,165, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> with 112,488.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-88">[88]</a></sup> Other religions have a small presence in Mississippi; as of 2010, there were 5,012 <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_United_States" title="Islam in the United States">Muslims</a>; 4,389 <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindus</a>; and 816 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD" title="Bahá'í">Bahá'í</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>Public opinion polls have consistently ranked Mississippi as the most religious state in the United States, with 59% of Mississippians considering themselves "very religious". The same survey also found that 11% of the population were non-Religious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> In a 2009 Gallup poll, 63% of Mississippians said that they attended church weekly or almost weekly – the highest percentage of all states (U.S. average was 42%, and the lowest percentage was in Vermont at 23%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Another 2008 Gallup poll found that 85% of Mississippians considered religion an important part of their daily lives, the highest figure among all states (U.S. average 65%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
