<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="LGBT">LGBT</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: LGBT">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Census,_2010" title="United States Census, 2010">2010 United States census</a> counted 6,286 same-sex unmarried-partner households in Mississippi, an increase of 1,512 since the 2000 United States census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LGBTCensus_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-LGBTCensus-97">[97]</a></sup> 33% contained at least one child, giving Mississippi the distinction of leading the nation in the percentage of same-sex couples raising children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Mississippi has the largest percentage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">African-American</a> same-sex couples among total households. The state capital, Jackson, ranks tenth in the nation in concentration of African-American same-sex couples. The state ranks fifth in the nation in the percentage of <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a> same-sex couples among all Hispanic households and ninth in the highest concentration of same-sex couples who are <a href="/wiki/Old_age" title="Old age">seniors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> With the passing of HB 1523 in April 2016, from July it became legal in Mississippi to refuse service to same-sex couples, based on one's religious beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>  The bill has become the subject of controversy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> A federal judge blocked the law in July,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> however it was challenged and a federal appeals court ruled in favor of the law in October 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The state is ranked 50th or last place among all the states for health care, according to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Fund" title="Commonwealth Fund">Commonwealth Fund</a>, a nonprofit foundation working to advance performance of the health care system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mississippi has the highest rate of infant and neonatal deaths of any U.S. state. Age-adjusted data also shows Mississippi has the highest overall death rate, and the highest death rate from heart disease, hypertension and hypertensive renal disease, influenza and pneumonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, Mississippi (and Arkansas) had the fewest dentists in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>For three years in a row, more than 30 percent of Mississippi's residents have been classified as obese. In a 2006 study, 22.8 percent of the state's children were classified as such. Mississippi had the <a href="/wiki/Obesity_in_the_United_States" title="Obesity in the United States">highest rate of obesity of any U.S. state</a> from 2005 to 2008, and also ranks first in the nation for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_blood_pressure" title="High blood pressure">high blood pressure</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sedentary_lifestyle" title="Sedentary lifestyle">adult inactivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> In a 2008 study of African-American women, contributing risk factors were shown to be: lack of knowledge about <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> (BMI), dietary behavior, physical inactivity and lack of social support, defined as motivation and encouragement by friends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> A 2002 report on African-American adolescents noted a 1999 survey which suggests that a third of children were obese, with higher ratios for those in the Delta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OATAAA_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-OATAAA-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The study stressed that "obesity starts in early childhood extending into the adolescent years and then possibly into adulthood." It noted impediments to needed behavioral modification, including the Delta likely being "the most underserved region in the state" with African Americans the major ethnic group; lack of accessibility and availability of medical care; and an estimated 60% of residents living below the poverty level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OATAAA_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-OATAAA-112">[112]</a></sup> Additional risk factors were that most schools had no physical education curriculum and nutrition education is not emphasized. Previous intervention strategies may have been largely ineffective due to not being culturally sensitive or practical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OATAAA_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-OATAAA-112">[112]</a></sup> A 2006 survey found nearly 95 percent of Mississippi adults considered childhood obesity to be a serious problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PPCOMA_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-PPCOMA-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mississippi_locations_by_per_capita_income" title="Mississippi locations by per capita income">Mississippi locations by per capita income</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_quarter,_reverse_side,_2002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="936" data-file-width="942" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mississippi_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2002.jpg/220px-Mississippi_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mississippi_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2002.jpg/330px-Mississippi_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mississippi_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2002.jpg/440px-Mississippi_quarter%2C_reverse_side%2C_2002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mississippi_quarter,_reverse_side,_2002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Mississippi <a href="/wiki/Quarter_(United_States_coin)" title="Quarter (United States coin)">U.S. quarter</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis" title="Bureau of Economic Analysis">Bureau of Economic Analysis</a> estimates that Mississippi's total state product in 2010 was $98 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> GDP growth was .5 percent in 2015 and is estimated to be 2.4 in 2016 according to Dr. Darrin Webb, the state's chief economist, who noted it would make two consecutive years of positive growth since the recession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pender,_2017_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pender,_2017-115">[115]</a></sup> Per capita personal income in 2006 was $26,908, the lowest per capita personal income of any state, but the state also has the nation's lowest living costs. 2015 data records the adjusted per capita personal income at $40,105.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pender,_2017_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pender,_2017-115">[115]</a></sup> Mississippians consistently rank as one of the highest per capita in charitable contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>At 56 percent, the state has one of the lowest workforce participation rates in the country. Approximately 70,000 adults are disabled which is 10 percent of the workforce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pender,_2017_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pender,_2017-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mississippi's rank as one of the poorest states is related to its dependence on cotton agriculture before and after the Civil War, late development of its frontier bottomlands in the Mississippi Delta, repeated natural disasters of flooding in the late 19th and early 20th century that required massive capital investment in levees, and ditching and draining the bottomlands, and slow development of railroads to link bottomland towns and river cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solomon_Final_Frontiers_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon_Final_Frontiers-117">[117]</a></sup> In addition, when Democrats regained control of the state legislature, they passed the 1890 constitution that discouraged corporate industrial development in favor of rural agriculture, a legacy that would slow the state's progress for years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the Civil War, Mississippi was the fifth-wealthiest state in the nation, its wealth generated by the labor of slaves in cotton plantations along the rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
Slaves were counted as property and the rise in the cotton markets since the 1840s had increased their value. By 1860, a majority – 55 percent – of the population of Mississippi was enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Ninety percent of the Delta bottomlands were undeveloped and the state had low overall density of population.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1048" data-file-width="1500" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg/220px-Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg/330px-Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg/440px-Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sharecroppers_daughter_Lauderdale_County_August_1935.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sharecropper's daughter, Lauderdale County, 1935</div></div></div>
<p>Largely due to the domination of the <a href="/wiki/Plantations_in_the_American_South" title="Plantations in the American South">plantation</a> economy, focused on the production of <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a> <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, the state's elite was reluctant to invest in infrastructure such as roads and railroads. They educated their children privately. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">Industrialization</a> did not reach many areas until the late 20th century. The planter <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a>, the elite of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antebellum_era" title="Antebellum era">antebellum</a> Mississippi, kept the tax structure low for their own benefit, making only private improvements. Before the war the most successful planters, such as <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> President <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a>, owned riverside properties along the Mississippi and Yazoo rivers in the Mississippi Delta. Away from the riverfronts, most of the Delta was undeveloped frontier.
</p><p>During the Civil War, 30,000 Mississippi soldiers, mostly white, died from wounds and disease, and many more were left crippled and wounded. Changes to the labor structure and an agricultural depression throughout the South caused severe losses in wealth. In 1860 assessed valuation of property in Mississippi had been more than $500 million, of which $218 million (43 percent) was estimated as the value of slaves. By 1870, total assets had decreased in value to roughly $177 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Poor whites and landless former slaves suffered the most from the postwar economic depression. The constitutional convention of early 1868 appointed a committee to recommend what was needed for relief of the state and its citizens. The committee found severe destitution among the laboring classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> It took years for the state to rebuild levees damaged in battles. The upset of the commodity system impoverished the state after the war. By 1868 an increased cotton crop began to show possibilities for free labor in the state, but the crop of 565,000 bales produced in 1870 was still less than half of prewar figures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Blacks cleared land, selling timber and developing bottomland to achieve ownership. In 1900, two-thirds of farm owners in Mississippi were blacks, a major achievement for them and their families. Due to the poor economy, low cotton prices and difficulty of getting credit, many of these farmers could not make it through the extended financial difficulties. Two decades later, the majority of African Americans were sharecroppers. The low prices of cotton into the 1890s meant that more than a generation of African Americans lost the result of their labor when they had to sell their farms to pay off accumulated debts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willis_Forgotten_Time_42-7"><a href="#cite_note-Willis_Forgotten_Time-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the Civil War, the state refused for years to build human capital by fully educating all its citizens. In addition, the reliance on agriculture grew increasingly costly as the state suffered loss of cotton crops due to the devastation of the <a href="/wiki/Boll_weevil" title="Boll weevil">boll weevil</a> in the early 20th century, devastating floods in 1912–1913 and 1927, collapse of cotton prices after 1920, and drought in 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solomon_Final_Frontiers_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Solomon_Final_Frontiers-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was not until 1884, after the flood of 1882, that the state created the Mississippi-Yazoo Delta District Levee Board and started successfully achieving longer term plans for levees in the upper Delta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about_the_levee_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-about_the_levee-24">[24]</a></sup> Despite the state's building and reinforcing levees for years, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a> broke through and caused massive flooding of 27,000 square miles (70,000 km<sup>2</sup>) throughout the Delta, homelessness for hundreds of thousands, and millions of dollars in property damages. With the Depression coming so soon after the flood, the state suffered badly during those years. In the <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">Great Migration</a>, hundreds of thousands of African Americans migrated North and West for jobs and chances to live as full citizens.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment_and_tourism">Entertainment and tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Entertainment and tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The legislature's 1990 decision to legalize casino gambling along the Mississippi River and the Gulf Coast has led to increased revenues and economic gains for the state. Gambling towns in Mississippi have attracted increased tourism: they include the Gulf Coast resort towns of <a href="/wiki/Bay_St._Louis,_Mississippi" title="Bay St. Louis, Mississippi">Bay St. Louis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulfport,_Mississippi" title="Gulfport, Mississippi">Gulfport</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biloxi,_Mississippi" title="Biloxi, Mississippi">Biloxi</a>, and the Mississippi River towns of <a href="/wiki/Tunica_Resorts,_Mississippi" title="Tunica Resorts, Mississippi">Tunica</a> (the third largest gaming area in the United States), <a href="/wiki/Greenville,_Mississippi" title="Greenville, Mississippi">Greenville</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg,_Mississippi" title="Vicksburg, Mississippi">Vicksburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez</a>.
</p><p>Before <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> struck the Gulf Coast, Mississippi was the second-largest gambling state in the Union, after <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a> and ahead of <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> An estimated $500,000 per day in tax revenue was lost following Hurricane Katrina's severe damage to several coastal casinos in Biloxi in August 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Because of the destruction from this hurricane, on October 17, 2005, Governor <a href="/wiki/Haley_Barbour" title="Haley Barbour">Haley Barbour</a> signed a bill into law that allows casinos in Hancock and Harrison counties to rebuild on land (but within 800 feet (240 m) of the water). The only exception is in <a href="/wiki/Harrison_County,_Mississippi" title="Harrison County, Mississippi">Harrison County</a>, where the new law states that casinos can be built to the southern boundary of <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_90" title="U.S. Route 90">U.S. Route 90</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 2012, Mississippi had the sixth largest gambling revenue of any state, with $2.25 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The federally recognized <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Band_of_Choctaw_Indians" title="Mississippi Band of Choctaw Indians">Mississippi Band of Choctaw Indians</a> has established a gaming casino on its reservation, which yields revenue to support education and economic development.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Momentum_Mississippi" title="Momentum Mississippi">Momentum Mississippi</a>, a statewide, public–private partnership dedicated to the development of economic and employment opportunities in Mississippi, was adopted in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing">Manufacturing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Manufacturing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs,_Mississippi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2400" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs%2C_Mississippi.jpg/220px-2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs%2C_Mississippi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs%2C_Mississippi.jpg/330px-2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs%2C_Mississippi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs%2C_Mississippi.jpg/440px-2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs%2C_Mississippi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2014_Toyota_Corolla_built_in_Blue_Springs,_Mississippi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2014 Toyota Corolla built by <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Motor_Manufacturing_Mississippi" title="Toyota Motor Manufacturing Mississippi">Toyota Motor Manufacturing Mississippi</a> on display at the <a href="/wiki/Tupelo_Automobile_Museum" title="Tupelo Automobile Museum">Tupelo Automobile Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mississippi, like the rest of its southern neighbors, is a <a href="/wiki/Right-to-work_law#U.S._states_with_right-to-work_laws" title="Right-to-work law">right-to-work state</a>. It has some major automotive factories, such as the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Motor_Manufacturing_Mississippi" title="Toyota Motor Manufacturing Mississippi">Toyota Mississippi Plant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Blue_Springs,_Mississippi" title="Blue Springs, Mississippi">Blue Springs</a> and a Nissan Automotive plant in <a href="/wiki/Canton,_Mississippi" title="Canton, Mississippi">Canton</a>. The latter produces the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Titan" title="Nissan Titan">Nissan Titan</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation">Taxation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Taxation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mississippi collects personal <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a> in three tax brackets, ranging from 3% to 5%. The retail <a href="/wiki/Sales_tax" title="Sales tax">sales tax</a> rate in Mississippi is 7%. Tupelo levies a local sales tax of 2.5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> State sales tax growth was 1.4 percent in 2016 and estimated to be slightly less in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pender,_2017_115-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pender,_2017-115">[115]</a></sup> For purposes of assessment for <a href="/wiki/Ad_valorem_tax" title="Ad valorem tax">ad valorem taxes</a>, taxable <a href="/wiki/Property_tax" title="Property tax">property</a> is divided into five classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>On August 30, 2007, a report by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> indicated that Mississippi was the poorest state in the country. Major cotton farmers in the Delta have large, mechanized plantations, and they receive the majority of extensive federal subsidies going to the state, yet many other residents still live as poor, rural, landless laborers. The state's sizable poultry industry has faced similar challenges in its transition from family-run farms to large mechanized operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Of $1.2 billion from 2002–2005 in federal subsidies to farmers in the Bolivar County area of the Delta, only 5% went to small farmers. There has been little money apportioned for rural development. Small towns are struggling. More than 100,000 people have left the region in search of work elsewhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The state had a median household income of $34,473.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of March 2016, the state's unemployment rate was 6.5%, the third highest in the country after <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_subsidies_and_spending">Federal subsidies and spending</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Federal subsidies and spending">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With Mississippi's fiscal conservatism, in which <a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">Medicaid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_welfare_state" title="American welfare state">welfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Nutrition_Assistance_Program" title="Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program">food stamps</a>, and other social programs are often cut, eligibility requirements are tightened, and stricter employment criteria are imposed, Mississippi ranks as having the second-highest ratio of spending to tax receipts of any state. In 2005, Mississippi citizens received approximately $2.02 per dollar of taxes in the way of federal spending. This ranks the state 2nd highest nationally, and represents an increase from 1995, when Mississippi received $1.54 per dollar of taxes in federal spending and was 3rd highest nationally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> This figure is based on federal spending after large portions of the state were devastated by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>, requiring large amounts of federal aid from the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Emergency_Management_Agency" title="Federal Emergency Management Agency">Federal Emergency Management Agency</a> (FEMA). However, from 1981 to 2005, it was at least number four in the nation for federal spending vs. taxes received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>A proportion of federal spending in Mississippi is directed toward large federal installations such as <a href="/wiki/Camp_Shelby" title="Camp Shelby">Camp Shelby</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Stennis_Space_Center" title="John C. Stennis Space Center">John C. Stennis Space Center</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Meridian" title="Naval Air Station Meridian">Meridian Naval Air Station</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Air_Force_Base" title="Columbus Air Force Base">Columbus Air Force Base</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Keesler_Air_Force_Base" title="Keesler Air Force Base">Keesler Air Force Base</a>. Three of these installations are located in the area affected by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_government">Politics and government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Politics and government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Mississippi" title="Government of Mississippi">Government of Mississippi</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Mississippi" title="List of Governors of Mississippi">List of Governors of Mississippi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1297" data-file-width="1279" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg/220px-Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg/330px-Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg/440px-Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Governors_of_Mississippi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Five Governors of Mississippi in 1976, from left to right: <a href="/wiki/Ross_Barnett" title="Ross Barnett">Ross Barnett</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_P._Coleman" title="James P. Coleman">James P. Coleman</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_L._Waller" title="William L. Waller">William L. Waller</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Bell_Williams" title="John Bell Williams">John Bell Williams</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paul_B._Johnson_Jr." title="Paul B. Johnson Jr.">Paul B. Johnson Jr.</a></div></div></div>
<p>As with all other U.S. states and the federal government, Mississippi's government is based on the separation of legislative, executive and judicial power. Executive authority in the state rests with the Governor, currently <a href="/wiki/Phil_Bryant" title="Phil Bryant">Phil Bryant</a> (R). The Lieutenant Governor, currently <a href="/wiki/Tate_Reeves" title="Tate Reeves">Tate Reeves</a> (R), is elected on a separate ballot. Both the governor and lieutenant governor are elected to four-year terms of office. Unlike the federal government, but like many other U.S. States, most of the heads of major executive departments are elected by the citizens of Mississippi rather than appointed by the governor.
</p><p>Mississippi is one of five states that elects its state officials in odd-numbered years (the others are <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>). Mississippi holds elections for these offices every four years, always in the year preceding Presidential elections.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws">Laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2004, Mississippi voters approved a state <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_amendment" title="Constitutional amendment">constitutional amendment</a> banning <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a> and prohibiting Mississippi from recognizing same-sex marriages performed elsewhere. The amendment passed 86% to 14%, the largest margin in any state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USAT1_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-USAT1-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSNBCgay_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSNBCgay-136">[136]</a></sup> Same-sex marriage became legal in Mississippi on June 26, 2015, when the United States Supreme Court invalidated all state-level bans on same-sex marriage as unconstitutional in the landmark case <a href="/wiki/Obergefell_v._Hodges" title="Obergefell v. Hodges">Obergefell v. Hodges</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mississippi_Gay_Marriage_Ban_Struck_Down_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mississippi_Gay_Marriage_Ban_Struck_Down-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mississippi is one of 31 states which have <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_the_United_States" title="Capital punishment in the United States">capital punishment</a> as a legal sentence (see <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment_in_Mississippi" title="Capital punishment in Mississippi">Capital punishment in Mississippi</a>).
</p><p>Section 265 of the Constitution of the State of Mississippi declares that "No person who denies the existence of a Supreme Being shall hold any office in this state."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mississippi_State_Constitution_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mississippi_State_Constitution-138">[138]</a></sup> This religious test restriction was held to be unconstitutional by the U.S. Supreme Court in <i><a href="/wiki/Torcaso_v._Watkins" title="Torcaso v. Watkins">Torcaso v. Watkins</a></i> (1961).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_alignment">Political alignment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Political alignment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi,_2016.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3201" data-file-width="2904" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi%2C_2016.svg/200px-United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi%2C_2016.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi%2C_2016.svg/300px-United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi%2C_2016.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi%2C_2016.svg/400px-United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi%2C_2016.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:United_States_presidential_election_in_Mississippi,_2016.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treemap" title="Treemap">Treemap</a> of the popular vote by county, 2016 presidential election.</div></div></div>
<p>Mississippi led the South in developing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after Reconstruction era">disfranchising</a> constitution, passing it in 1890. By raising barriers to voter registration, the state legislature disenfranchised most blacks and many poor whites, excluding them from politics until the late 1960s. It established a one-party state dominated by white Democrats.
</p><p>In the 1980s the whites divided evenly between the parties. In the 1990s they voters shifted their allegiance to the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a>, first for national and then for state offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>. Most blacks were still <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disfranchisement_after_Reconstruction_era" title="Disfranchisement after Reconstruction era">disenfranchised</a> under the state's 1890 constitution and discriminatory practices, until passage of the <a href="/wiki/Voting_Rights_Act_of_1965" title="Voting Rights Act of 1965">Voting Rights Act of 1965</a> and concerted grassroots efforts to achieve registration and encourage voting.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air">Air</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Air">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Mississippi has six airports with commercial passenger service, the busiest in Jackson (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jackson-Evers_International_Airport" title="Jackson-Evers International Airport">Jackson-Evers International Airport</a>) and one in Gulfport (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulfport-Biloxi_International_Airport" title="Gulfport-Biloxi International Airport">Gulfport-Biloxi International Airport</a>)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Roads">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1218" data-file-width="1701" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg/220px-Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg/330px-Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg/440px-Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vicksburg_bridge_over_the_mississippi_morning-edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Vicksburg_Bridge" title="Vicksburg Bridge">Vicksburg Bridge</a> carries I-20 and U.S. 80 across the Mississippi River at Vicksburg.</div></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Rail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Passenger">Passenger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Passenger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a> provides scheduled passenger service along two routes, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crescent_(Amtrak)" title="Crescent (Amtrak)">Crescent</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/City_of_New_Orleans_(train)" title="City of New Orleans (train)">City of New Orleans</a></i>. Prior to severe damage from <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunset_Limited" title="Sunset Limited">Sunset Limited</a></i> traversed the far south of the state; the route originated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles, California</a> and it terminated in <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Freight">Freight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Freight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>All but two of the United States <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_I_railroad" title="Class I railroad">Class I railroads</a> serve Mississippi (the exceptions are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Union_Pacific" title="Union Pacific">Union Pacific</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canadian_Pacific" title="Canadian Pacific">Canadian Pacific</a>):
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Canadian_National_Railway" title="Canadian National Railway">Canadian National Railway</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Central_Railroad" title="Illinois Central Railroad">Illinois Central Railroad</a> subsidiary provides north-south service.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/BNSF_Railway" title="BNSF Railway">BNSF Railway</a> has a northwest-southeast line across northern Mississippi.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Southern_Railway" title="Kansas City Southern Railway">Kansas City Southern Railway</a> provides east-west service in the middle of the state and north-south service along the <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> state line.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Norfolk_Southern_Railway" title="Norfolk Southern Railway">Norfolk Southern Railway</a> provides service in the extreme north and southeast.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/CSX_Transportation" title="CSX Transportation">CSX</a> has a line along the Gulf Coast.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water">Water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_rivers">Major rivers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Major rivers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>

<li><a href="/wiki/Big_Black_River_(Mississippi)" title="Big Black River (Mississippi)">Big Black River</a></li>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_bodies_of_water">Major bodies of water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Major bodies of water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg/220px-Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg/330px-Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg/440px-Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ross_Barnett_Reservoir_sunset_picture.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ross Barnett Reservoir at sunset.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arkabutla_Lake" title="Arkabutla Lake">Arkabutla Lake</a> – 19,550 acres (79.1 km<sup>2</sup>) of water; constructed and managed by the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers Vicksburg District<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bay_Springs_Lake" title="Bay Springs Lake">Bay Springs Lake</a> – 6,700 acres (27 km<sup>2</sup>) of water and 133 miles (214 km) of shoreline; constructed and managed by the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grenada_Lake" title="Grenada Lake">Grenada Lake</a> – 35,000 acres (140 km<sup>2</sup>) of water; became operational in 1954; constructed and managed by the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers Vicksburg District<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ross_Barnett_Reservoir" title="Ross Barnett Reservoir">Ross Barnett Reservoir</a> – Named for <a href="/wiki/Ross_Barnett" title="Ross Barnett">Ross Barnett</a>, the 52nd <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Mississippi" title="List of Governors of Mississippi">Governor of Mississippi</a>; 33,000 acres (130 km<sup>2</sup>) of water; became operational in 1966; constructed and managed by The Pearl River Valley Water Supply District, a state agency; Provides water supply for the City of Jackson.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sardis_Lake_(Mississippi)" title="Sardis Lake (Mississippi)">Sardis Lake</a> – 98,520 acres (398.7 km<sup>2</sup>) of water; became operational in October 1940; constructed and managed by the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers Vicksburg District<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Enid_Lake" title="Enid Lake">Enid Lake</a> – 44,000 acres (180 km<sup>2</sup>) of water; constructed and managed by the U.S. Army</li></ul>
<p><a class="external free" href="http://www.mvk.usace.army.mil/Missions/Recreation/Enid-Lake/" rel="nofollow">http://www.mvk.usace.army.mil/Missions/Recreation/Enid-Lake/</a>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Mississippi_media" title="Category:Mississippi media">Category:Mississippi media</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_Mississippi" title="List of colleges and universities in Mississippi">List of colleges and universities in Mississippi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Mississippi" title="Education in Mississippi">Education in Mississippi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School(Negro)%22-Tupelo,_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1343" data-file-width="2000" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School%28Negro%29%22-Tupelo%2C_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif/lossy-page1-220px-NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School%28Negro%29%22-Tupelo%2C_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School%28Negro%29%22-Tupelo%2C_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif/lossy-page1-330px-NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School%28Negro%29%22-Tupelo%2C_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School%28Negro%29%22-Tupelo%2C_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif/lossy-page1-440px-NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School%28Negro%29%22-Tupelo%2C_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NYA-%22Lee_County_Training_School(Negro)%22-Tupelo,_Mississippi-students_at_work_in_library_-_NARA_-_195369.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>School students in their library, Tupelo, Mississippi, 1936</div></div></div>
<p>Until the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a> era, Mississippi had a small number of schools and no educational institutions for African Americans. The first school for black students was not established until 1862.
</p><p>During Reconstruction in 1871, black and white Republicans drafted a constitution that was the first to provide for a system of free <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_education" title="Public education">public education</a> in the state. The state's dependence on agriculture and resistance to taxation limited the funds it had available to spend on any schools. In the early 20th century, there were still few schools in rural areas, particularly for black children. With seed money from the <a href="/wiki/Julius_Rosenwald" title="Julius Rosenwald">Julius Rosenwald</a> Fund, many rural black communities across Mississippi raised matching funds and contributed public funds to build new schools for their children. Essentially, many black adults taxed themselves twice and made significant sacrifices to raise money for the education of children in their communities, in many cases donating land and/or labor to build such schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Blacks and whites attended segregated and separate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_school_(government_funded)" title="Public school (government funded)">public schools</a> in Mississippi until the late 1960s, although such segregation had been declared unconstitutional by the United States Supreme Court in its 1954 ruling in <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a></i>. In the majority-black Mississippi Delta counties, white parents worked through <a href="/wiki/Citizens%27_Councils" title="Citizens' Councils">White Citizens' Councils</a> to set up private <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Segregation_academies" title="Segregation academies">segregation academies</a>, where they enrolled their children. Often funding declined for the public schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bolton178_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-bolton178-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>But in the state as a whole, only a small minority of white children were withdrawn from public schools. State officials believed they needed to maintain public education to attract new businesses. After several years of integration, whites often dominated local systems anyway, maintaining white supremacy. Many black parents complained that they had little representation in school administration, and that many of their former administrators and teachers had been pushed out. They have had to work to have their interests and children represented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bolton178_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-bolton178-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 1980s, the state had 954 public elementary and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_school" title="Secondary school">secondary schools</a>, with a total yearly enrollment of about 369,500 elementary pupils and about 132,500 secondary students. Some 45,700 students attended <a href="/wiki/Private_school" title="Private school">private schools</a>. 
</p><p>In the 21st century, 91% of white children in the state attend public schools and most of the black children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In 2008, Mississippi was ranked last among the fifty states in academic achievement by the <a href="/wiki/American_Legislative_Exchange_Council" title="American Legislative Exchange Council">American Legislative Exchange Council</a>'s <i>Report Card on Education</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alec_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-alec-146">[146]</a></sup> with the lowest average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ACT_(examination)" title="ACT (examination)">ACT</a> scores and sixth-lowest spending per pupil in the nation. In contrast, Mississippi had the 17th-highest average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SAT_(examination)" title="SAT (examination)">SAT</a> scores in the nation. As an explanation, the Report noted that 92% of Mississippi high school graduates took the ACT, but only 3% of graduates took the SAT, apparently a self-selection of higher achievers. This breakdown compares to the national average of high school graduates taking the ACT and SAT, of 43% and 45%, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alec_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-alec-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although unusual in the West, <a href="/wiki/School_corporal_punishment" title="School corporal punishment">school corporal punishment</a> is common in Mississippi, with 31,236 public school students<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> paddled at least one time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CORPUN_US_SCHOOL_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-CORPUN_US_SCHOOL-148">[148]</a></sup> A greater percentage of students were <a href="/wiki/Paddle_(spanking)" title="Paddle (spanking)">paddled</a> in Mississippi than in any other state, according to government data for the 2011–2012 school year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CORPUN_US_SCHOOL_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-CORPUN_US_SCHOOL-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, Mississippi students scored the lowest of any state on the National Assessments of Educational Progress in both math and science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jackson,_MS" title="Jackson, MS">Jackson</a>, the state's capital city, is the site of the state residential school for deaf and hard of hearing students. <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_School_for_the_Deaf" title="Mississippi School for the Deaf">The Mississippi School for the Deaf</a> was established by the state legislature in 1854 before the civil war.
</p><p>The Mississippi School for Mathematics and Science (MSMS) is a public residential high school for academically gifted students. It is located in <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Mississippi" title="Columbus, Mississippi">Columbus, Mississippi</a> on the campus of the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_University_for_Women" title="Mississippi University for Women">Mississippi University for Women</a>. MSMS was founded in 1987 by appropriations from the Mississippi Legislature and it is the fourth public, residential high school for academically gifted students in the United States.[3] The school enrolls students only in the last two years of high school. Rising tenth-grade students from across the state apply and are selected on a competitive basis.
</p><p>The Mississippi School of the Arts (MSA) is an upper high school of literary, visual, and performing arts on the historic <a href="/wiki/Whitworth_Female_College" title="Whitworth Female College">Whitworth College</a> Campus in <a href="/wiki/Brookhaven,_Mississippi" title="Brookhaven, Mississippi">Brookhaven, Mississippi</a>, about sixty miles (97 km) south of Jackson, Mississippi.[1] MSA teaches 11th and 12th grade students. The campus has six buildings designated as Mississippi Landmarks, and is itself an historic district listed on the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a>.[1]
</p><p>The Mississippi School of the Arts provides advanced, residential programs of study in visual arts, vocal music, theatre, dance, and literary arts for "artistically gifted" 11th/12th grade students from throughout Mississippi.[3][1] The comprehensive residential and academic curriculum prepares students for further studies or to pursue employment. Some non-arts courses (some math, science, etc.) are taught in conjunction with Brookhaven High School, 6 blocks away, to provide a wider curriculum.[1] Students apply for admission during their sophomore year.[1]
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While Mississippi has been especially known for its music and literature, it has embraced other forms of art. Its strong religious traditions have inspired striking works by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outsider_Art" title="Outsider Art">outsider artists</a> who have been shown nationally.
</p><p>Jackson established the <a href="/wiki/USA_International_Ballet_Competition" title="USA International Ballet Competition">USA International Ballet Competition</a>, which is held every four years. This ballet competition attracts the most talented young dancers from around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Magnolia_Independent_Film_Festival" title="Magnolia Independent Film Festival">Magnolia Independent Film Festival</a>, still held annually in <a href="/wiki/Starkville,_Mississippi" title="Starkville, Mississippi">Starkville</a>, is the first and oldest in the state.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Ohr" title="George Ohr">George Ohr</a>, known as the "Mad Potter of Biloxi" and the father of abstract expressionism in pottery, lived and worked in Biloxi, MS.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mississippi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Musicians of the state's Delta region were historically significant to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">blues</a>. Although by the end of the 19th century, two-thirds of the farm owners were black, continued low prices for cotton and national financial pressures resulted in most of them losing their land. More problems built up with the boll weevil infestation, when thousands of agricultural jobs were lost.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jimmie_Rodgers_(country_singer)" title="Jimmie Rodgers (country singer)">Jimmie Rodgers</a>, a native of Meridian and guitarist/singer/songwriter known as the "Father of Country Music", played a significant role in the development of the blues. He and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chester_Arthur_Burnett" title="Chester Arthur Burnett">Chester Arthur Burnett</a> were friends and admirers of each other's music. Their friendship and respect is an important example of Mississippi's musical legacy. While the state has had a reputation for being the most racist in the United States, individual musicians created an integrated music community. Mississippi musicians created new forms by combining and creating variations on musical traditions from Africa with the musical traditions of white Southerners, a tradition largely rooted in Scots–Irish music.
</p><p>The state is creating a <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Blues_Trail" title="Mississippi Blues Trail">Mississippi Blues Trail</a>, with dedicated markers explaining historic sites significant to the history of blues music, such as <a href="/wiki/Clarksdale,_Mississippi" title="Clarksdale, Mississippi">Clarksdale</a>'s Riverside Hotel, where <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Smith" title="Bessie Smith">Bessie Smith</a> died after her auto accident on <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_61" title="U.S. Route 61">Highway 61</a>. The Riverside Hotel is just one of many historical blues sites in Clarksdale. The <a href="/wiki/Delta_Blues_Museum" title="Delta Blues Museum">Delta Blues Museum</a> there is visited by tourists from all over the world. Close by is "Ground Zero", a contemporary blues club and restaurant co-owned by actor <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Freeman" title="Morgan Freeman">Morgan Freeman</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley" title="Elvis Presley">Elvis Presley</a>, who created a sensation in the 1950s as a crossover artist and contributed to rock 'n' roll, was a native of <a href="/wiki/Tupelo,_Mississippi" title="Tupelo, Mississippi">Tupelo</a>. From opera star <a href="/wiki/Leontyne_Price" title="Leontyne Price">Leontyne Price</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative rock</a> band <a href="/wiki/3_Doors_Down" title="3 Doors Down">3 Doors Down</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_and_western_(music_genre)" title="Gulf and western (music genre)">gulf and western</a> singer <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Buffett" title="Jimmy Buffett">Jimmy Buffett</a>, modern rock/jazz/world music guitarist-producer <a href="/wiki/Clifton_Hyde" title="Clifton Hyde">Clifton Hyde</a>, to rappers <a href="/wiki/David_Banner" title="David Banner">David Banner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Big_K.R.I.T." title="Big K.R.I.T.">Big K.R.I.T.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afroman" title="Afroman">Afroman</a>, Mississippi musicians have been significant in all genres.
</p>
